\section{Analytical Section}

\subsection{Q1(a): Forward Pass}
Given
\[
X=
\begin{bmatrix}
1 & 2 & 0\\
0 & 1 & 1\\
1 & 0 & 2
\end{bmatrix},
\qquad
W=
\begin{bmatrix}
1 & 0\\
-1 & 1
\end{bmatrix},
\qquad
b=0
\]
with stride $=1$ and no padding.

The pre-activation feature map is:
\[
z_{11} =
\begin{bmatrix}
1 & 2\\
0 & 1
\end{bmatrix}
\odot
\begin{bmatrix}
1 & 0\\
-1 & 1
\end{bmatrix}
= 2
\]
\[
z_{12} =
\begin{bmatrix}
2 & 0\\
1 & 1
\end{bmatrix}
\odot
\begin{bmatrix}
1 & 0\\
-1 & 1
\end{bmatrix}
= 2
\]
\[
z_{21} =
\begin{bmatrix}
0 & 1\\
1 & 0
\end{bmatrix}
\odot
\begin{bmatrix}
1 & 0\\
-1 & 1
\end{bmatrix}
= -1
\]
\[
z_{22} =
\begin{bmatrix}
1 & 1\\
0 & 2
\end{bmatrix}
\odot
\begin{bmatrix}
1 & 0\\
-1 & 1
\end{bmatrix}
= 3
\]

Hence,
\[
Z=
\begin{bmatrix}
2 & 2\\
-1 & 3
\end{bmatrix}
\]

Applying ReLU,
\[
A=\text{ReLU}(Z)=
\begin{bmatrix}
2 & 2\\
0 & 3
\end{bmatrix}
\]

Flattening:
\[
A_{\text{flat}}=
\begin{bmatrix}
2\\
2\\
0\\
3
\end{bmatrix}
\]

Given
\[
V=[1,-1,2,0.5], \qquad c=1
\]
the output is
\[
Y=V\cdot A_{\text{flat}}+c
\]
\[
Y=(1)(2)+(-1)(2)+(2)(0)+(0.5)(3)+1=2.5
\]

Given target $T=3$, the loss is
\[
L=\frac{1}{2}(Y-T)^2
=\frac{1}{2}(2.5-3)^2
=0.125
\]

Therefore,
\[
\boxed{
Z=
\begin{bmatrix}
2 & 2\\
-1 & 3
\end{bmatrix},
\quad
A=
\begin{bmatrix}
2 & 2\\
0 & 3
\end{bmatrix},
\quad
Y=2.5,
\quad
L=0.125
}
\]

\subsection{Q1(b): Backward Pass}
We first compute
\[
\frac{\partial L}{\partial Y}=Y-T=2.5-3=-0.5
\]

\subsubsection*{Gradient with respect to $V$}
Since
\[
Y=V\cdot A_{\text{flat}}+c
\]
we have
\[
\frac{\partial L}{\partial V}
=
\frac{\partial L}{\partial Y}\frac{\partial Y}{\partial V}
=
(-0.5)A_{\text{flat}}
\]
\[
\frac{\partial L}{\partial V}
=
(-0.5)
\begin{bmatrix}
2\\2\\0\\3
\end{bmatrix}
=
\begin{bmatrix}
-1\\-1\\0\\-1.5
\end{bmatrix}
\]
So,
\[
\boxed{
\frac{\partial L}{\partial V}=[-1,-1,0,-1.5]
}
\]

\subsubsection*{Gradient with respect to $A_{\text{flat}}$}
\[
\frac{\partial L}{\partial A_{\text{flat}}}
=
\frac{\partial L}{\partial Y}\frac{\partial Y}{\partial A_{\text{flat}}}
=
(-0.5)[1,-1,2,0.5]
\]
\[
\frac{\partial L}{\partial A_{\text{flat}}}
=
[-0.5,0.5,-1,-0.25]
\]

Reshaping:
\[
\frac{\partial L}{\partial A}
=
\begin{bmatrix}
-0.5 & 0.5\\
-1 & -0.25
\end{bmatrix}
\]

\subsubsection*{Backpropagation through ReLU}
\[
Z=
\begin{bmatrix}
2 & 2\\
-1 & 3
\end{bmatrix}
\Rightarrow
\text{ReLU}'(Z)=
\begin{bmatrix}
1 & 1\\
0 & 1
\end{bmatrix}
\]

Thus
\[
\frac{\partial L}{\partial Z}
=
\frac{\partial L}{\partial A}\odot \text{ReLU}'(Z)
=
\begin{bmatrix}
-0.5 & 0.5\\
0 & -0.25
\end{bmatrix}
\]

\subsubsection*{Gradient with respect to $W$}
Each entry of $Z$ corresponds to a $2\times2$ patch of $X$.

Contribution from $z_{11}$:
\[
-0.5
\begin{bmatrix}
1 & 2\\
0 & 1
\end{bmatrix}
=
\begin{bmatrix}
-0.5 & -1\\
0 & -0.5
\end{bmatrix}
\]

Contribution from $z_{12}$:
\[
0.5
\begin{bmatrix}
2 & 0\\
1 & 1
\end{bmatrix}
=
\begin{bmatrix}
1 & 0\\
0.5 & 0.5
\end{bmatrix}
\]

Contribution from $z_{21}$:
\[
0
\begin{bmatrix}
0 & 1\\
1 & 0
\end{bmatrix}
=
\begin{bmatrix}
0 & 0\\
0 & 0
\end{bmatrix}
\]

Contribution from $z_{22}$:
\[
-0.25
\begin{bmatrix}
1 & 1\\
0 & 2
\end{bmatrix}
=
\begin{bmatrix}
-0.25 & -0.25\\
0 & -0.5
\end{bmatrix}
\]

Adding all contributions:
\[
\frac{\partial L}{\partial W}
=
\begin{bmatrix}
-0.5 & -1\\
0 & -0.5
\end{bmatrix}
+
\begin{bmatrix}
1 & 0\\
0.5 & 0.5
\end{bmatrix}
+
\begin{bmatrix}
0 & 0\\
0 & 0
\end{bmatrix}
+
\begin{bmatrix}
-0.25 & -0.25\\
0 & -0.5
\end{bmatrix}
\]
\[
\boxed{
\frac{\partial L}{\partial W}
=
\begin{bmatrix}
0.25 & -1.25\\
-0.5 & -0.5
\end{bmatrix}
}
\]

\subsection{Q1(c): Interpretation}
The weight that changes the most under gradient descent is the one with the largest absolute gradient:
\[
\left|\frac{\partial L}{\partial W}\right|=
\begin{bmatrix}
0.25 & 1.25\\
0.5 & 0.5
\end{bmatrix}
\]
Hence, $w_{12}$ changes the most since
\[
\frac{\partial L}{\partial w_{12}}=-1.25
\]
Under gradient descent,
\[
w_{12}^{\text{new}}=w_{12}-\eta(-1.25)=w_{12}+1.25\eta
\]
so $w_{12}$ increases.

Structurally, this happens because $w_{12}$ participates in multiple receptive fields due to weight sharing, aligns with relatively large input values, and receives gradient only from spatial positions where the ReLU is active. Therefore, its contributions accumulate most strongly.

\subsection{Q3(a): Global Max Pooling}
With
\[
A=
\begin{bmatrix}
2 & 2\\
0 & 3
\end{bmatrix}
\]
global max pooling gives
\[
Y=\max(A)=3
\]
Only the maximum activation $a_{22}=3$ receives gradient. Therefore, only the pixels in its receptive field can have nonzero gradient:
\[
X_{2,2}, X_{2,3}, X_{3,2}, X_{3,3}
\]
All other pixels have zero gradient:
\[
X_{1,1}, X_{1,2}, X_{1,3}, X_{2,1}, X_{3,1}
\]

\subsection{Q3(b): Layer Normalization}
For
\[
A=
\begin{bmatrix}
2 & 2\\
0 & 3
\end{bmatrix}
\]
the mean is
\[
\mu = \frac{2+2+0+3}{4}=1.75
\]
and the variance is
\[
\sigma^2=\frac{(2-1.75)^2+(2-1.75)^2+(0-1.75)^2+(3-1.75)^2}{4}=1.1875
\]
Hence,
\[
A_{\text{norm}}=\frac{A-\mu}{\sqrt{\sigma^2}}
\approx
\begin{bmatrix}
0.2294 & 0.2294\\
-1.6059 & 1.1471
\end{bmatrix}
\]

\subsection{Q3(c): Interpretation}
Max pooling provides translation invariance because it preserves only the largest activation in a region. If an input feature shifts slightly in space but still produces the same maximum activation, the pooled output remains unchanged. Therefore, small translations in the input may not significantly affect the final prediction or loss.

\section{Programming Section}

\subsection{Task 1 Part A: Standard MNIST}

\subsubsection*{Model Design}
A custom CNN was designed under the assignment constraint of fewer than 50,000 trainable parameters. The final model contained 26,698 trainable parameters and consisted of:
\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=1.5em]
    \item two convolutional layers,
    \item two max-pooling layers,
    \item one hidden fully connected layer,
    \item one output layer for 10-way digit classification.
\end{itemize}

\subsubsection*{Results}
The model achieved:
\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=1.5em]
    \item Final training accuracy: 98.71\%
    \item Final validation accuracy: 98.48\%
    \item Test accuracy: 98.56\%
\end{itemize}

The close match between train, validation, and test accuracy indicates strong generalization and minimal overfitting.

\subsubsection*{Analysis of Loss Curves}
The training and validation losses decreased steadily over epochs, showing effective convergence. The small gap between train and validation performance suggests that the model learned meaningful digit features without memorizing the training data.

\subsubsection*{Analytical Question 1.1}
The model generalized well. Both training and validation curves remained close throughout training, and the final performance gap was very small. This indicates that the network did not suffer from severe overfitting or underfitting.

\subsubsection*{Analytical Question 1.2}
The first-layer filters capture basic visual primitives such as edges, stroke patterns, and simple intensity transitions. Some filters resemble horizontal, vertical, or diagonal edge detectors, while others appear to capture parts of handwritten curves and corners. These low-level patterns serve as the basis for deeper layers to form more abstract representations of digit structure.

\subsection{Task 1 Part B: Colored-MNIST}

\subsubsection*{Results}
The Colored-MNIST model achieved:
\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=1.5em]
    \item Biased test accuracy: 98.81\%
    \item Unbiased test accuracy: 81.67\%
\end{itemize}

This large gap clearly shows shortcut learning. The model relied on color-label correlation present in the biased training data, but performance dropped significantly when evaluated on unbiased data where that spurious correlation no longer held.

\subsubsection*{Analytical Question 1.3}
The optimizer preferred color over shape because color was an easier and more immediately predictive feature in the biased training set. Since gradient descent updates parameters to reduce training loss as quickly as possible, the network learned to exploit color as a shortcut. As a result, it performed very well on the biased test set but generalized poorly to the unbiased test set.

\subsubsection*{Analytical Question 1.4}
To force the network to focus on shape rather than color, several strategies could be used:
\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=1.5em]
    \item convert images to grayscale,
    \item randomize colors during training,
    \item balance the dataset so color is not correlated with label,
    \item train across multiple environments with varying color-label relationships,
    \item use regularization or invariance-promoting methods.
\end{itemize}

\subsubsection*{Curve Interpretation}
Although the training and validation loss and accuracy curves were excellent, they were measured on the same biased distribution as training. Therefore, they overstated the model's true generalization ability. The drop in performance on the unbiased test set reveals that high validation accuracy alone is not sufficient when the validation data shares the same shortcut.

\subsection{Task 2: Transfer Learning and Interpretability}

\subsubsection*{Fine-Tuning ResNet-18 on STL-10}
A pretrained ResNet-18 model was used as the backbone. All convolutional layers were frozen, and only the final fully connected layer was replaced and trained for the 10 STL-10 classes.

\subsubsection*{Result}
The model achieved a best test accuracy of:
\[
\boxed{94.24\%}
\]

\subsubsection*{Analytical Question 2.1}
Freezing the early layers is beneficial because those layers already encode generic visual features such as edges, textures, and simple shapes learned from large-scale ImageNet pretraining. These representations transfer well to STL-10. Training only the classification head reduces computational cost, shortens training time, and lowers the risk of overfitting on a relatively small dataset.

\subsubsection*{Training Behavior}
The ResNet-18 model showed stable convergence:
\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=1.5em]
    \item Training accuracy improved from 75.34\% to 94.84\%
    \item Test accuracy improved from 91.37\% to 94.24\%
    \item Test loss decreased from 0.4093 to 0.1980
\end{itemize}

    \centering
    \begin{subfigure}{0.48\linewidth}

\end{figure}

\subsubsection*{GradCAM Analysis}
GradCAM was used to interpret the model's predictions by visualizing the image regions that most influenced the decision.

\paragraph{Analytical Question 2.2}
For correctly classified samples, the heatmaps focus on semantically meaningful regions of the object. In the horse example, the model concentrates on the body of the horse. In the monkey example, the strongest attention lies on the monkey's face and upper body. This indicates that the network is using relevant object-level features for classification.

\paragraph{Analytical Question 2.3}
For incorrectly classified samples, the model attends to misleading or incomplete cues. In the truck misclassified as a car, the attention focuses on the front structure and wheels, which are visually similar to a car. In the airplane misclassified as a ship, the heatmap emphasizes the water and surrounding scene rather than the airplane itself. This suggests that errors arise when the model relies on partial local features or context-based background cues instead of full object understanding.

This assignment demonstrated both analytical understanding and practical implementation of deep learning models. In the analytical section, the Toy-CNN forward and backward passes were computed manually, illustrating convolution, ReLU, gradient flow, max pooling, and normalization. In the programming section, the MNIST model generalized well, the Colored-MNIST experiment exposed shortcut learning through spurious color correlations, and transfer learning with ResNet-18 on STL-10 achieved strong performance while GradCAM provided interpretability into correct and incorrect predictions.

\section{Analytical Section}

\subsection{Q1(a): Forward Pass}

\[
Z=
\begin{bmatrix}
2 & 2\\
-1 & 3
\end{bmatrix}
\quad
A=
\begin{bmatrix}
2 & 2\\
0 & 3
\end{bmatrix}
\]

\[
Y=2.5, \quad L=0.125
\]

\subsection{Q1(b): Backward Pass}

\[
\frac{\partial L}{\partial V} = [-1,-1,0,-1.5]
\]

\[
\frac{\partial L}{\partial W} =
\begin{bmatrix}
0.25 & -1.25\\
-0.5 & -0.5
\end{bmatrix}
\]

\subsection{Q1(c)}
The weight \( w_{12} \) has the largest gradient magnitude and changes the most. This is because it participates in multiple receptive fields and aligns with strong activations.

\subsection{Q3(a)}
Only the maximum activation receives gradient; all other positions get zero gradient.

\subsection{Q3(b)}
\[
\mu = 1.75, \quad \sigma^2 = 1.1875
\]

\subsection{Q3(c)}
Max pooling provides translation invariance by preserving dominant features regardless of small shifts.

\section{Analytical Section}

\subsection{Q1(a): Forward Pass}
\[
Z=
\begin{bmatrix}
2 & 2\\
-1 & 3
\end{bmatrix},
\quad
A=
\begin{bmatrix}
2 & 2\\
0 & 3
\end{bmatrix},
\quad
Y=2.5,
\quad
L=0.125
\]

\subsection{Q1(b): Backward Pass}
\[
\frac{\partial L}{\partial V} = [-1,-1,0,-1.5]
\]
\[
\frac{\partial L}{\partial W} =
\begin{bmatrix}
0.25 & -1.25\\
-0.5 & -0.5
\end{bmatrix}
\]

\subsection{Q1(c)}
The weight \(w_{12}\) changes the most because it has the largest gradient magnitude and receives strong contributions from multiple receptive fields.

\subsection{Q3}
For max pooling, only the maximum activation receives gradient, giving translation invariance. For layer normalization:
\[
\mu = 1.75, \qquad \sigma^2 = 1.1875
\]

\section{Task 1: CNN Models}

\subsection{MNIST}
The custom CNN contained 26,698 trainable parameters and achieved:
\begin{itemize}
    \item Train Accuracy: 98.71\%
    \item Validation Accuracy: 98.48\%
    \item Test Accuracy: 98.56\%
\end{itemize}
The model generalized well because training, validation, and test performance were all very close.

\begin{figure}[H]
\centering
\includegraphics[width=0.6\linewidth]{mnist_loss.png}
\caption{MNIST loss curve}
\end{figure}

\begin{figure}[H]
\centering
\includegraphics[width=0.6\linewidth]{mnist_accuracy.png}
\caption{MNIST accuracy curve}
\end{figure}

\begin{figure}[H]
\centering
\includegraphics[width=0.6\linewidth]{mnist_filters.png}
\caption{First-layer filters learned on MNIST}
\end{figure}

The first-layer filters capture low-level visual features such as edges, stroke patterns, and intensity transitions.

\subsection{Colored-MNIST}
The Colored-MNIST model achieved:
\begin{itemize}
    \item Biased Test Accuracy: 98.81\%
    \item Unbiased Test Accuracy: 81.67\%
\end{itemize}

This large drop shows shortcut learning. The model relied on color-label correlation rather than true digit shape.

\begin{figure}[H]
\centering
\includegraphics[width=0.6\linewidth]{colored_mnist_loss.png}
\caption{Colored-MNIST loss curve}
\end{figure}

\begin{figure}[H]
\centering
\includegraphics[width=0.6\linewidth]{colored_mnist_accuracy.png}
\caption{Colored-MNIST accuracy curve}
\end{figure}

To encourage the network to focus on shape, possible strategies include grayscale conversion, color randomization, balanced data, and training across multiple environments.

\section{Task 2: Transfer Learning on STL-10}

A pretrained ResNet-18 was used with the convolutional backbone frozen and only the final classification layer trained.

\[
\textbf{Best Test Accuracy = 94.24\%}
\]

Freezing early layers is effective because they already contain general visual features such as edges, textures, and shapes learned from ImageNet. Training only the last layer reduces computation and helps prevent overfitting.

\begin{figure}[H]
\centering
\includegraphics[width=0.6\linewidth]{STL-10 ResNet-18 Loss.png}

\end{figure}

\caption{STL-10 ResNet-18 accuracy curve}
\end{figure}

\section{Analytical Section}

\subsection{Q1(a): Forward Pass}
\[
Z=
\begin{bmatrix}
2 & 2\\
-1 & 3
\end{bmatrix},
\quad
A=
\begin{bmatrix}
2 & 2\\
0 & 3
\end{bmatrix},
\quad
Y=2.5,
\quad
L=0.125
\]

\subsection{Q1(b): Backward Pass}
\[
\frac{\partial L}{\partial V} = [-1,-1,0,-1.5]
\]
\[
\frac{\partial L}{\partial W} =
\begin{bmatrix}
0.25 & -1.25\\
-0.5 & -0.5
\end{bmatrix}
\]

\subsection{Q1(c)}
The weight \(w_{12}\) changes the most because it has the largest gradient magnitude and receives strong contributions from multiple receptive fields.

\subsection{Q3}
For max pooling, only the maximum activation receives gradient, giving translation invariance. For layer normalization:
\[
\mu = 1.75, \qquad \sigma^2 = 1.1875
\]

\section{Task 1: CNN Models}

\subsection{MNIST}
The custom CNN contained 26,698 trainable parameters and achieved:
\begin{itemize}
    \item Train Accuracy: 98.71\%
    \item Validation Accuracy: 98.48\%
    \item Test Accuracy: 98.56\%
\end{itemize}

\begin{figure}[H]
\centering
\includegraphics[width=0.6\linewidth]{mnist_loss.png}
\caption{MNIST loss curve}
\end{figure}

\begin{figure}[H]
\centering
\includegraphics[width=0.6\linewidth]{mnist_accuracy.png}
\caption{MNIST accuracy curve}
\end{figure}

\begin{figure}[H]
\centering
\includegraphics[width=0.6\linewidth]{mnist_filters.png}
\caption{First-layer filters learned on MNIST}
\end{figure}

The first-layer filters capture low-level visual features such as edges, stroke patterns, and intensity transitions.

\subsection{Colored-MNIST}
The Colored-MNIST model achieved:
\begin{itemize}
    \item Biased Test Accuracy: 98.81\%
    \item Unbiased Test Accuracy: 81.67\%
\end{itemize}

This large drop shows shortcut learning. The model relied on color-label correlation rather than true digit shape.

\begin{figure}[H]
\centering
\includegraphics[width=0.6\linewidth]{colored_mnist_loss.png}
\caption{Colored-MNIST loss curve}
\end{figure}

\begin{figure}[H]
\centering
\includegraphics[width=0.6\linewidth]{colored_mnist_accuracy.png}
\caption{Colored-MNIST accuracy curve}
\end{figure}

To encourage the network to focus on shape, possible strategies include grayscale conversion, color randomization, balanced data, and training across multiple environments.

\section{Task 2: Transfer Learning on STL-10}

A pretrained ResNet-18 was used with the convolutional backbone frozen and only the final classification layer trained.

\[
\textbf{Best Test Accuracy = 94.24\%}
\]

Freezing early layers is effective because they already contain general visual features such as edges, textures, and shapes learned from ImageNet. Training only the last layer reduces computation and helps prevent overfitting.

\begin{figure}[H]
\centering
\includegraphics[width=0.6\linewidth]{stl10_loss.png}
\caption{STL-10 ResNet-18 loss curve}
\end{figure}

\begin{figure}[H]
\centering
\includegraphics[width=0.6\linewidth]{stl10_accuracy.png}
\caption{STL-10 ResNet-18 accuracy curve}
\end{figure}

% =========================
\section{Task 1: Analytical Understanding}

\subsection{Forward Pass (Convolution + ReLU)}

The convolution operation extracts spatial features by applying learnable filters across the input. The activation matrix obtained after convolution is:

\[
Z=
\begin{bmatrix}
2 & 2\\
-1 & 3
\end{bmatrix}
\]

Applying ReLU activation:

\[
A=\max(0,Z)=
\begin{bmatrix}
2 & 2\\
0 & 3
\end{bmatrix}
\]

The final prediction is:
\[
Y = 2.5
\]
\[
L = 0.125
\]

\subsection{Backward Pass}

Gradients are computed using the chain rule. The gradients with respect to parameters are:

\[
\frac{\partial L}{\partial V} = [-1,-1,0,-1.5]
\]

\[
\frac{\partial L}{\partial W} =
\begin{bmatrix}
0.25 & -1.25\\
-0.5 & -0.5
\end{bmatrix}
\]

\subsection{Interpretation}

The parameter \(w_{12}\) has the largest magnitude gradient. This indicates it contributes most to the loss and will be updated more aggressively during optimization.

\subsection{Pooling and Normalization}

Max pooling propagates gradients only through the maximum activation, which introduces translation invariance. For layer normalization:

\[
\mu = 1.75, \quad \sigma^2 = 1.1875
\]

This stabilizes training by normalizing feature distributions.

% =========================
\section{Task 2: CNN on MNIST}

\subsection{Model Description}

A convolutional neural network was designed consisting of:
\begin{itemize}
\item Two convolutional layers
\item ReLU activations
\item Max pooling layers
\item Fully connected classifier
\end{itemize}

\subsection{Results}

\begin{itemize}
\item Train Accuracy: 98.71\%
\item Validation Accuracy: 98.48\%
\item Test Accuracy: 98.56\%
\end{itemize}

\subsection{Analysis}

The close alignment between training and validation accuracy indicates strong generalization. The model successfully learned digit shapes and patterns.

\begin{figure}[H]
\centering

\subsection{Filter Interpretation}

\begin{figure}[H]
\centering
\includegraphics[width=0.6\linewidth]{mnist_filters.png}
\caption{Learned Filters}
\end{figure}

The filters capture low-level features such as edges, strokes, and contours, which are essential for digit recognition.

% =========================
\section{Task 3: Colored-MNIST and Shortcut Learning}

\subsection{Results}

\begin{itemize}
\item Biased Accuracy: 98.81\%
\item Unbiased Accuracy: 81.67\%
\end{itemize}

\subsection{Analysis of Shortcut Learning}

The model relies heavily on color as a predictive feature. Since color is correlated with labels in training data, the model learns a spurious relationship.

\subsection{Why Performance Drops}

When evaluated on unbiased data:
\begin{itemize}
\item Color-label correlation is broken
\item Model fails to generalize
\item Accuracy drops significantly
\end{itemize}

\subsection{Solutions}

\begin{itemize}
\item Data augmentation (random colors)
\item Grayscale conversion
\item Multi-environment training
\item Regularization techniques
\end{itemize}

\begin{figure}[H]
\centering
\includegraphics[width=0.6\linewidth]{colored_mnist_loss.png}
\caption{Colored-MNIST Loss}
\end{figure}

\begin{figure}[H]
\centering
\includegraphics[width=0.6\linewidth]{colored_mnist_accuracy.png}
\caption{Colored-MNIST Accuracy}
\end{figure}

% =========================
\section{Task 4: Transfer Learning on STL-10}

\subsection{Methodology}

A pretrained ResNet-18 model was used:
\begin{itemize}
\item Frozen convolutional layers
\item Trained only final fully connected layer
\end{itemize}

\subsection{Results}

\[
\textbf{Test Accuracy = 94.24\%}
\]

\subsection{Why Transfer Learning Works}

\begin{itemize}
\item Early layers learn universal features (edges, textures)
\item Reduces training time
\item Prevents overfitting
\end{itemize}

\subsection{Comparison with Training from Scratch}

\begin{itemize}
\item Faster convergence
\item Higher accuracy
\item Better generalization
\end{itemize}

\begin{figure}[H]
\centering
\includegraphics[width=0.6\linewidth]{stl10_loss.png}

% =========================
\section{Summary of All Tasks}

This assignment combined analytical understanding of convolutional neural networks with practical deep learning implementation. In the analytical section, the Toy-CNN was studied through explicit forward and backward pass calculations. This developed an understanding of convolution, ReLU activation, gradient flow, pooling, and normalization. In particular, the manual derivations demonstrated how local receptive fields and weight sharing influence both activations and gradients.

In the programming section, the first task required building a compact CNN for standard MNIST under architectural constraints. The model achieved strong performance with closely matched training, validation, and test accuracy, indicating good generalization. The same architecture was then adapted for Colored-MNIST, where the gap between biased and unbiased accuracy revealed shortcut learning. This experiment showed that neural networks can exploit spurious correlations, such as color, instead of learning the true robust feature, which in this case was digit shape.

The second programming task used transfer learning with a pretrained ResNet-18 on STL-10. By freezing the backbone and training only the final classification head, the model achieved strong accuracy efficiently. GradCAM was then used to interpret model decisions. For correct predictions, the heatmaps showed attention on the main object, while for incorrect predictions the attention often shifted to misleading background regions or partial structures. Overall, the assignment illustrated three important ideas: low-level feature learning in CNNs, shortcut learning under dataset bias, and the usefulness of transfer learning and interpretability methods in practical computer vision.

% =========================
\section{Analytical Section}

\subsection{Q1: Forward and Backward Pass}

\subsubsection*{Q1(a) Forward Pass}

The input image and filter are:
\[
X =
\begin{bmatrix}
1 & 2 & 0 \\
0 & 1 & 1 \\
1 & 0 & 2
\end{bmatrix},
\qquad
W =
\begin{bmatrix}
1 & 0 \\
-1 & 1
\end{bmatrix}
\]

Using stride 1 and no padding, the convolution output is:
\[
Z =
\begin{bmatrix}
2 & 2 \\
-1 & 3
\end{bmatrix}
\]

Applying ReLU:
\[
A =
\begin{bmatrix}
2 & 2 \\
0 & 3
\end{bmatrix}
\]

Flattening gives:
\[
A_{\text{flat}} = [2,2,0,3]
\]

Using
\[
V = [1,-1,2,0.5], \qquad c = 1
\]
the prediction is:
\[
Y = (1)(2)+(-1)(2)+(2)(0)+(0.5)(3)+1 = 2.5
\]

The loss is:
\[
L = \frac{1}{2}(Y-T)^2 = \frac{1}{2}(2.5-3)^2 = 0.125
\]

\subsubsection*{Q1(b) Backward Pass}

First,
\[
\frac{\partial L}{\partial Y} = Y-T = -0.5
\]

Gradient with respect to fully connected weights:
\[
\frac{\partial L}{\partial V} = [-1,-1,0,-1.5]
\]

Gradient with respect to the convolution filter:
\[
\frac{\partial L}{\partial W} =
\begin{bmatrix}
0.25 & -1.25 \\
-0.5 & -0.5
\end{bmatrix}
\]

\subsubsection*{Q1(c) Interpretation}

The weight \(w_{12}\) would change the most because it has the largest gradient magnitude:
\[
\left|\frac{\partial L}{\partial w_{12}}\right| = 1.25
\]
Since its gradient is negative, gradient descent increases this weight. Structurally, this happens because that filter position participates in multiple receptive fields and receives strong contributions from active spatial locations in the forward pass.

% =========================
\subsection{Q3: Pooling and Normalization}

\subsubsection*{Q3(a) Max Pooling}

With global max pooling applied directly to
\[
A=
\begin{bmatrix}
2 & 2 \\
0 & 3
\end{bmatrix}
\]
the output becomes:
\[
Y = \max(A)=3
\]

Only the maximum activation receives gradient, so the backward flow becomes sparse. Pixels outside the receptive field of that maximum activation have zero gradient.

\subsubsection*{Q3(b) Normalization}

The mean of the feature map is:
\[
\mu = \frac{2+2+0+3}{4} = 1.75
\]

The variance is:
\[
\sigma^2 = 1.1875
\]

Thus, the normalized feature map is:
\[
A_{\text{norm}} = \frac{A-\mu}{\sqrt{\sigma^2}}
\]

\subsubsection*{Q3(c) Interpretation}

Max pooling provides translation invariance because the output depends only on the maximum activation. If the feature shifts slightly but the same maximum value remains in the pooling region, the output and therefore the loss remain unchanged.

% =========================
\section{Programming Section}

\subsection{Task 1: Custom CNNs and Shortcut Learning}

\subsubsection*{Part A: Standard MNIST}

A custom CNN was implemented for MNIST under the assignment constraints. The final model had 26,698 trainable parameters, satisfying the requirement of staying below 50,000.

The model achieved:
\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=1.5em]
    \item Training Accuracy: 98.71\%
    \item Validation Accuracy: 98.48\%
    \item Test Accuracy: 98.56\%
\end{itemize}

\paragraph{Analytical Question 1.1}
The model generalized well. The training and validation curves were very close, and there was no large gap between them. This indicates that the model learned useful digit features without severe overfitting or underfitting.

\paragraph{Analytical Question 1.2}
The first-layer filters captured basic visual primitives such as edges, strokes, and simple intensity transitions. This is expected in CNNs, since early layers typically learn low-level features that later layers combine into more complex digit representations.

\begin{figure}[H]
\centering
\includegraphics[width=0.65\linewidth]{mnist_loss.png}
\caption{MNIST training and validation loss curves}
\end{figure}

\begin{figure}[H]
\centering
\includegraphics[width=0.65\linewidth]{mnist_accuracy.png}
\caption{MNIST training and validation accuracy curves}
\end{figure}

\begin{figure}[H]
\centering
\includegraphics[width=0.65\linewidth]{mnist_filters.png}
\caption{First-layer convolutional filters learned by the MNIST CNN}
\end{figure}

% =========================
\subsubsection*{Part B: Colored-MNIST}

The same CNN was adapted to accept RGB input and trained on the Colored-MNIST dataset.

The model achieved:
\begin{itemize}[leftmargin=1.5em]
    \item Biased Test Accuracy: 98.81\%
    \item Unbiased Test Accuracy: 81.67\%
\end{itemize}

\paragraph{Analytical Question 1.3}
The large drop in performance on the unbiased test set shows that the model relied on the spurious correlation between color and label. During optimization, color acted as an easier predictive feature than shape, so gradient descent favored it because it reduced the training loss more quickly.

\paragraph{Analytical Question 1.4}
To encourage the model to focus on shape rather than color, one could use grayscale conversion, random color augmentation, better-balanced datasets, or multi-environment training where color is not consistently correlated with label.

\begin{figure}[H]
\centering
\includegraphics[width=0.65\linewidth]{colored_mnist_loss.png}
\caption{Colored-MNIST training and validation loss curves}
\end{figure}

\begin{figure}[H]
\centering
\includegraphics[width=0.65\linewidth]{colored_mnist_accuracy.png}
\caption{Colored-MNIST training and validation accuracy curves}
\end{figure}

% =========================
\subsection{Task 2: Transfer Learning and Interpretability}

\subsubsection*{Part A: Fine-Tuning ResNet-18 on STL-10}

A pretrained ResNet-18 was loaded from \texttt{torchvision.models}. All backbone layers were frozen, and the final fully connected layer was replaced with a new 10-class classifier for STL-10.

The model achieved:
\[
\textbf{Test Accuracy = 94.24\%}
\]

\paragraph{Analytical Question 2.1}
Freezing the early layers is computationally beneficial because it reduces the number of trainable parameters and speeds up training. It is also functionally beneficial because early layers in pretrained networks usually capture general features such as edges, textures, and simple shapes, which transfer well across datasets. Later layers are more task-specific and therefore the final classifier is adapted to the new task.

\begin{figure}[H]
\centering
\includegraphics[width=0.65\linewidth]{stl10_loss.png}
\caption{STL-10 ResNet-18 training and test loss curves}
\end{figure}

\begin{figure}[H]
\centering
\includegraphics[width=0.65\linewidth]{stl10_accuracy.png}
\caption{STL-10 ResNet-18 training and test accuracy curves}
\end{figure}

% =========================
\subsubsection*{Part B: Visualizing Decisions with GradCAM}

GradCAM was used to inspect which regions of the image contributed most strongly to the model's decision.

\paragraph{Analytical Question 2.2}
For correctly classified images, the heatmaps show that the model focuses on the primary object. This suggests that the classifier is using relevant object-level features rather than relying mainly on background context.

\begin{figure}[H]
\centering
\includegraphics[width=0.45\linewidth]{gradcam_outputs/correct1_original.png}
\includegraphics[width=0.45\linewidth]{gradcam_outputs/correct1_gradcam.png}

\vspace{0.3cm}

\includegraphics[width=0.45\linewidth]{gradcam_outputs/correct2_original.png}
\includegraphics[width=0.45\linewidth]{gradcam_outputs/correct2_gradcam.png}
\caption{GradCAM visualizations for correct predictions}
\end{figure}

\paragraph{Analytical Question 2.3}
For incorrectly classified images, the heatmaps show attention on misleading regions such as background context or only partial parts of the object. This suggests that the model makes mistakes when it relies on incomplete visual evidence or confusing contextual features rather than the full object structure.

\begin{figure}[H]
\centering
\includegraphics[width=0.45\linewidth]{gradcam_outputs/incorrect1_original.png}
\includegraphics[width=0.45\linewidth]{gradcam_outputs/incorrect1_gradcam.png}

\vspace{0.3cm}

\includegraphics[width=0.45\linewidth]{gradcam_outputs/incorrect2_original.png}
\includegraphics[width=0.45\linewidth]{gradcam_outputs/incorrect2_gradcam.png}
\caption{GradCAM visualizations for incorrect predictions}
\end{figure}

\section{Conclusion}

This assignment connected mathematical understanding with practical deep learning implementation. The Toy-CNN analysis illustrated the mechanics of forward and backward propagation. The MNIST model showed strong generalization, while the Colored-MNIST experiment revealed shortcut learning through spurious correlations. Finally, transfer learning with ResNet-18 achieved strong performance on STL-10, and GradCAM provided interpretability into both successful and failed predictions.
